<span id="freeTextContainer6101594171781033189">9/10<br/><br/>Following the publication of <i>Tales from Earthsea</i> that marked her triumphant return to the world of Earthsea, continuing the stories of the characters who entranced the hearts of children and adults alike and were loved by millions of fans, Ursula Le Guin brings in the World Fantasy Award-winning <i>The Other Wind</i>, the sixth and final book of the Earthsea Cycle, the classic series to its culmination, taking us in a story of kings, princesses, wizards, and dragons; but also of life and death, cho</span>
  <span id="freeText6101594171781033189" style="display:none">9/10<br /><br />Following the publication of <i>Tales from Earthsea</i> that marked her triumphant return to the world of Earthsea, continuing the stories of the characters who entranced the hearts of children and adults alike and were loved by millions of fans, Ursula Le Guin brings in the World Fantasy Award-winning <i>The Other Wind</i>, the sixth and final book of the Earthsea Cycle, the classic series to its culmination, taking us in a story of kings, princesses, wizards, and dragons; but also of life and death, choice and change, and joining and freedom, in a magical end of a saga.<br /><br />In the beginning of time, mankind and dragons were one kind, speaking one language; until their peoples agreed to part and dragons chose wildness and freedom in the west, while human beings, renegating on their agreement, sought wealth and power in the east...<br /><br />However today, having passed fifteen years sing the coronation of the young King Lebannen and the coming of peace in the Archipelago, something in changing in Earthsea, disturbing the boundaries between life and death and threatening to endanger the Equilibrium entire â€“ but Alder, having been born by a witch in the town of Elini on Taon, the Isle of the Harpers, and sailing from one island to the next, has been standing in the prow of <i>Farflyer</i> as it glides towards Gont Port.<br /><br />But when â€“ after trudging up the long road to Re Albi and the Old Mageâ€™s house â€“ he turns to the ex-Archmage, telling him of the haunting dreams that torment his sleep and his fear of them and asking him for his counsel, Alder will depart soon with Sparrowhawkâ€™s encouragement to Havnor, where Tenar and Tehanu are, seeking in the Kingâ€™s City those who can help him.<br /><br />Meanwhile, Lebannen, after five years of difficult and tiresome diplomacy with the new High King of the Kargs, will deal with the common interests of their realms; while Tenar, having travelled to Havnor with Tehanu to stand by his side and support him as a mother would do, will be plunged into politics of the court.<br /><br />Nevertheless, with the change on the laws of life and death to have caused a turmoil in Earthsea, triggering the dragons to break their long truce with the lands of the east and the multitudes of the dead to be searching for a way to break free, when Alder, Tenar, Tehanu, and the young King Lebannen along with Orm Irian and the High Princess of the Kargad Lands take the decision to sail to Roke Island, and seek with the counsel of the Masters of Earthsea for a solution to the worldâ€™s problems, all of them will find themselves standing on the edge of the precipice, bringing them before an ancient wrong â€“ an ancient wrong which, if they fail to break what was built and make the world whole, everything they knew and loved could be destroyed forever.<br /><br />Picking up some years after the end of the novel <i>Tehanu</i> and the short story <i>Dragonfly</i>, linking their stories and characters together and building the plot toward the final end, Ursula Le Guin travels us one more time to Earthsea, taking us in <i>The Other Wind</i> to the island of Gont, where the busy streets of Gont Port are full of markets and crowds, the peak of Gont Mountain overhungs like a cloud, and where in the little village of Re Albi the small houses are scattered among rocky pastures; to Havnor, the Great City, where in the Palace of Maharion â€“ rebuilt after centuries â€“ kings and queens built their lineages, in the throne room, Morredâ€™s High Seat, where the Kingâ€™s Council takes place, and in the highest where the Sword of Erreth-Akbe is set; to Roke Island, where in the Great House the Masters of Earthsea teach the High Arts, in the Immanent Grove where the heart of peace is, and in Roke Knoll where all the powers of earth meet; and lastly to the dry land, the dark land, where the souls of the dead go, languishing forever in the shadows, and where nothing changes.<br /><br /><i>The Other Wind</i> is the most complex novel of the series. A novel in which Le Guin, bringing all the characters we loved from the very beginning, taking us with them on a final journey and bringing to a close their tales, raises the bar higher than before, crafting through her mythmaking and warm writing a story both complex and magical.<br /><br />A final novel which, although it doesnâ€™t have the grand adventures of the original trilogy, focuses on the humans and the dragons of Earthsea, delving into their peoples and their common history, while at the same time explores themes of death, immortality and reincarnation, as well as the choices and fears the characters have to face, that build towards the culmination of the book, and close this magical journey wonderfully and meaningfully.<br /><br />In conclusion, <i>The Other Wind</i> is a wonderful final book, with Ursula Le Guin bringing back all that we loved, traveling us for one last time to the world and the characters of Earthsea, and culminating through her warm and humane writing this saga beautifully.<br /><br /><br /><br />*Î•Î»Î»Î·Î½Î¹ÎºÎ® ÎºÏÎ¹Ï„Î¹ÎºÎ®:<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" <div id="review_3101965163" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3101965163" />
    <a title="Irene" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36459924-irene"><img alt="Irene" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589091840p2/36459924.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3101965163?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Irene" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Irene" href="/user/show/36459924-irene">Irene</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781842552117" href="/book/show/222106.The_Other_Wind">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36459924-irene?shelf=fantasies-similar-to-earthsea">fantasies-similar-to-earthsea</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3101965163" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5429230010830030958">Where do I go from here? I've finished the last three books of the Earthsea Cycle and just don't know what to do with myself. Big sigh out. There's nothing to say about The Other Wind except that we've come full circle. It's not one person, Ged or Tenar, but everyone that's the hero. It's not a person of great renowned, a king or mage, but an ordinary man that will mend what was whole. How far we've come from A Wizard of Earthsea - a spellbinding classic. Every single volume of the Earthsea Cycl</span>
  <span id="freeText5429230010830030958" style="display:none">Where do I go from here? I've finished the last three books of the Earthsea Cycle and just don't know what to do with myself. Big sigh out. There's nothing to say about The Other Wind except that we've come full circle. It's not one person, Ged or Tenar, but everyone that's the hero. It's not a person of great renowned, a king or mage, but an ordinary man that will mend what was whole. How far we've come from A Wizard of Earthsea - a spellbinding classic. Every single volume of the Earthsea Cycle is nothing short of iconic. Breaking with reader expectations of what a fantasy series could be, each story was like a bread crumb trail in the night and Ursula K. Le Guin led us into the full force of daylight in The Other Wind.<br><br>Le Guin asks so many questions about magic in her adult trilogy of Earthsea (The Farthest Shore, Tales From Earthsea, The Other Wind): Who gets to have magic and what does that say about who gets to have power? What sacrifices do we make in the pursuit of power, and at what cost are those sacrifices worth it? What is a hero? What is a noble life well lived? Is life worth living without love? <br><br>The Other Wind, again, is a mature rumination on these and many more questions. I love the paradoxes and riddles Earthsea plays with, and the adult trilogy of Earthsea truly exemplifies the push and pull dynamics of the Equilibrium. <br><br>It doesn't get more opposite in The Other Wind. There is no central protagonist but a cast of male and female characters, magical and non-magical that all take centre stage. If we had to say a central character in terms of galvanizing the plot, of being the catalyst, well it's a minor sorcerer who then loses his powers that... well I am NOT going to say anything more than that other than to echo what other reviewers have noted that The Other Wind ties up the loose strands, whispered and teased at starting in Tehanu and "Dragonfly" in Tales From Earthsea. <br><br>The first time I read The Farthest Shore Cob seemed so random a villain that I was actually surprised finding out the villain Ged and Arren were looking for was Cob, the one and the same that had already been mentioned by Ged in passing earlier on. If Tehanu injected the Cob storyline with a bit of scary suspense that made Cob's whole deal feel real, The Other Wind makes the Why of the Cob storyline matter. <br><br>One thing that struck out to me about The Other Wind was that if Tehanu was about looking at Earthsea from that of rural Gont then The Other Wind is about the mundane machinations of politics and court life. I absolutely loved the scenes with Lebannen ruling his kingdom. Every scene between Lebannen and the mere mention of the Princess was hilarious - Le Guin has a knack for writing hero and heroines with chemistry that leap off the page. <br><br>The Other Wind is so poignant, lyrical. It was the perfect send-off to the world of Earthsea but I didn't want it to end. I nearly cried when I read the last page - I don't know how Le Guin did that because it was Tenar and Ged talking about the every day. I guess it's because the reader has known them as liminal figures that fought with the forces of light and dark in the prime of their lives, and now to end with that final glimpse of them in their fifties, still as much in love with each other as before, continuing to carry on with their lives farming in what used to be their teacher's abode...<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1501658351ra/23480808.gif" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>That feeling when you finish a book but you can't let go? That's me right now. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5429230010830030958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_771237645" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/771237645" />
    <a title="Nimue Brown" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6091697-nimue-brown"><img alt="Nimue Brown" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442613846p2/6091697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/771237645?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nimue Brown" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nimue Brown" href="/user/show/6091697-nimue-brown">Nimue Brown</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer771237645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5710462514961273560">If you haven't read the other Earthsea books, don't start here. It may make sense as a standalone but will be much the poorer as a read if you aren't rooted in the characters and the world already. This is a rich, complex setting, and much of the joy in this tale revolves around the re-imagining of that which perhaps you thought you already knew about this land. If you don't have a sense of Earthsea already, much of the plot will bear less significance, be less interesting and make less sense.<br/><br/>We</span>
  <span id="freeText5710462514961273560" style="display:none">If you haven't read the other Earthsea books, don't start here. It may make sense as a standalone but will be much the poorer as a read if you aren't rooted in the characters and the world already. This is a rich, complex setting, and much of the joy in this tale revolves around the re-imagining of that which perhaps you thought you already knew about this land. If you don't have a sense of Earthsea already, much of the plot will bear less significance, be less interesting and make less sense.<br /><br />Weaving together so many strands from the previous Earthsea tales, this is a beautiful imaginative and inspiring story. Of the set it perhaps says most about our relationships with life and death. A very human sort of book, for all the dragons and magic it contains. A story about life and being alive. I wanted more. I wanted pages of insight into all of the characters, and much more detail on pretty much everything that happened. I wanted it to be five times the length, at least. What this means is that I will have to imagine the rest, and that is part of what makes it such a good book. There are many stories to keep inventing for yourself long after you've stopped reading. Very much recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5710462514961273560" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2163943740" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2163943740" />
    <a title="Bryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36632319-bryan"><img alt="Bryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581439647p2/36632319.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2163943740?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryan" href="/user/show/36632319-bryan">Bryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/20204640-the-other-wind">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36632319-bryan?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36632319-bryan?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2163943740" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12369428698576463752">Thus, it is complete. For now anyway. Le Guin claims this is the end of the cycle, that <i>this</i> is Earthsea. But I don't know if I believe her. Maybe I just don't want to believe her. I'm not sure she can stay away, and I'm thinking (hoping) that someday soon we'll see a return to the Archipelago, and the magic of Earthsea. <br/><br/>When I first read A Wizard of Earthsea, not long ago really, I commented on the Balance of the world. A balance that must be carefully maintained. Over the course of this series</span>
  <span id="freeText12369428698576463752" style="display:none">Thus, it is complete. For now anyway. Le Guin claims this is the end of the cycle, that <i>this</i> is Earthsea. But I don't know if I believe her. Maybe I just don't want to believe her. I'm not sure she can stay away, and I'm thinking (hoping) that someday soon we'll see a return to the Archipelago, and the magic of Earthsea. <br /><br />When I first read A Wizard of Earthsea, not long ago really, I commented on the Balance of the world. A balance that must be carefully maintained. Over the course of this series that balance has been skewed, slowly but surely. In The Other Wind an attempt is made to finally restore that Balance. And it is a good story. <br /><br />I'm leaving this one with 4 stars, though I think it would also fit the 3.5 that I've left the previous two with. I enjoy these stories, and I'm glad to have read them. But there was always something keeping me from a 5 star rating. I really can't put my finger on it, but I feel it. It's something about the way the story is told. I've tried to put it into words in my other reviews of the series but found that I couldn't. I still can't, but this is my last change to try. The closest I can get is to say that it feels almost impersonal at times, like you're too far <i>above</i> the characters and situations playing out. As I said though, I can't really put what I mean into words, so this isn't entirely accurate. Especially because I feel like I can easily contradict myself by staying that these stories are <i>very</i> personal at times. The stories of Ged, Tenar, Lebannen, Tehanu, Alder, Ogion, Irian, and the rest <i>are</i> personal ones. So what do I mean? I'm really not sure. Maybe I will be able to better articulate it after some future reread. But for now know that I am satisfied with my stay in Earthsea, and hope to return.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12369428698576463752" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('H/K3rUIcXImEKzZMnlS5xwn3pvdZMz6oFAoGTZhbIXQLMzcbM8++XsQlkMG99QeZXvKVSzsLcKHLLVZHeERQVA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('DeJLOYJBG7Yt9Rn3/z8peLsJGxGLMnNI2n09LhN2TIgZI8uP85L5YW37v3rcnpcm7AworekKPUEFWm0k82k9qA==')})
  }

The conduct of the marquise shows precisely enough religious devotion to attain under a new Maintenon to the gloomy piety of the last days of Louis XIV., and enough worldliness to adopt the habits of gallantry of the first years of that reign, should it ever be revived. Stepping<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3232942620611134708\" style=\"display:none\">Back to the city, or back to nature? Seattle author David Williams shows us how we can get the best of both. Botany and bugs, geology and geese, and creeks and crows; living in a major city doesn\'t have to separate us from the natural world. Stepping away from a guidebook format, Williams presents the reader with a series of essays and maps that weave personal musings, bits of humor, natural history observations, and scientific data into a multi-textured perspective of life in the city--descriptions of his journeys as a naturalist in an urban landscape. Williams addresses questions that an observant person asks in an urban environment. What did Seattle look like before Europeans got here? How does the area\'s geologic past affect us? Why have some animals thrived and other languished? How are we affected by the species with whom we share the urban environment and how do we affect them? This book captures all of the distinctive flavors of the Emerald City, urban and natural.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"3232942620611134708\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/874602.Ursula_K_Le_Guin">About Ursula K. Le Guin</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/874602.Ursula_K_Le_Guin"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1244291425p3/874602.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/874602.Ursula_K_Le_Guin">Ursula K. Le Guin
</a></div>

18,053
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4739142910034079899">Ursula K. Le Guin published twenty-two novels, eleven volumes of short stories, four collections of essays, twelve books for children, six volumes of poetry and four of translation, and has received many awards: Hugo, Nebula, National Book Award, PEN-Malamud, etc. Her recent publications include the novel <i>Lavinia</i>, an essay collection, <i>Cheek by Jowl</i>, and <i>The Wild Girls</i>. She lived in Portland, Orego</span>
  <span id="freeText4739142910034079899" style="display:none">Ursula K. Le Guin published twenty-two novels, eleven volumes of short stories, four collections of essays, twelve books for children, six volumes of poetry and four of translation, and has received many awards: Hugo, Nebula, National Book Award, PEN-Malamud, etc. Her recent publications include the novel <i>Lavinia</i>, an essay collection, <i>Cheek by Jowl</i>, and <i>The Wild Girls</i>. She lived in Portland, Oregon.<br /><br />She was known for her treatment of gender (<i>The Left Hand of Darkness</i>, <i>The Matter of Seggri</i>), political systems (<i>The Telling</i>, <i>The Dispossessed</i>) and difference/otherness in any other form. Her interest in non-Western philosophies was reflected in works such as "Solitude" and <i>The Telling</i> but even more interesting are her imagined societies, often mixing traits extracted from her profound knowledge of anthropology acquired from growing up with her father, the famous anthropologist, Alfred Kroeber. The Hainish Cycle reflects the anthropologist's experience of immersing themselves in new strange cultures since most of their main characters and narrators (Le Guin favoured the first-person narration) are envoys from a humanitarian organization, the Ekumen, sent to investigate or ally themselves with the people of a different world and learn their ways.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4739142910034079899" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
